"Roman Baroque" <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Bacchus" title="Temple of Bacchus">Temple of Bacchus</a> at <a href="/wiki/Baalbek" title="Baalbek">Baalbek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roman_temple" title="Roman temple">Roman temple</a></div>
<p>Roman temples were among the most important and richest buildings in Roman culture, though only a few survive in any sort of complete state. Their construction and maintenance was a major part of <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Rome" title="Religion in ancient Rome">ancient Roman religion</a>, and all towns of any importance had at least one main temple, as well as smaller shrines. The main room <i>(cella)</i> housed the <a href="/wiki/Cult_(religious_practice)" title="Cult (religious practice)">cult</a> image of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_deities" title="List of Roman deities">deity</a> to whom the temple was <a href="/wiki/Votum" title="Votum">dedicated</a>, and often a small altar for incense or <a href="/wiki/Libation" title="Libation">libations</a>. Behind the cella was a room or rooms used by temple attendants for storage of equipment and offerings.</p>
<p>Some remains of many Roman temples survive, above all in Rome itself, but the relatively few near-complete examples were nearly all converted to Christian churches (and sometimes subsequently to <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosques</a>), usually a considerable time after the initial <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great_and_Christianity" title="Constantine the Great and Christianity">triumph of Christianity under Constantine</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Decline_of_Greco-Roman_polytheism" title="Decline of Greco-Roman polytheism">decline of Roman religion</a> was relatively slow, and the temples themselves were not appropriated by the government until a decree of the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Honorius_(emperor)" title="Honorius (emperor)">Honorius</a> in 415. Some of the oldest surviving temples include the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Hercules_Victor" title="Temple of Hercules Victor">Temple of Hercules Victor</a> (mid 2nd century BC) and <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Portunus" title="Temple of Portunus">Temple of Portunus</a> (120-80 BC), both standing within the <a href="/wiki/Forum_Boarium" title="Forum Boarium">Forum Boarium</a>.</p>
<p>The form of the Roman temple was mainly derived from the <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_architecture" title="Etruscan architecture">Etruscan</a> model, but using Greek styles. Roman temples emphasised the front of the building, which followed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_temple" title="Greek temple">Greek temple</a> models and typically consisted of wide steps leading to a <a href="/wiki/Portico" title="Portico">portico</a> with columns, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pronaos" title="Pronaos">pronaos</a>, and usually a triangular <a href="/wiki/Pediment" title="Pediment">pediment</a> above, which was filled with statuary in the most grand examples; this was as often in <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracotta</a> as stone, and no examples have survived except as fragments. However, unlike the Greek models, which generally gave equal treatment to all sides of the temple, which could be viewed and approached from all directions, the sides and rear of Roman temples might be largely undecorated (as in the <a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Pantheon, Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_temple_of_Vic" title="Roman temple of Vic">Vic</a>), inaccessible by steps (as in the <a href="/wiki/Maison_Carr%C3%A9e" title="Maison Carrée">Maison Carrée</a> and Vic), and even back on to other buildings. As in the Maison Carrée, columns at the side might be half-columns, emerging from ("engaged with" in architectural terminology) the wall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The platform on which the temple sat was typically raised higher in Roman examples than Greek, with up ten or twelve or more steps rather than the three typical in Greek temples; the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Claudius" title="Temple of Claudius">Temple of Claudius</a> was raised twenty steps. These steps were normally only at the front, and typically not the whole width of that.</p>
<p>The Greek <a href="/wiki/Classical_order" title="Classical order">classical orders</a> in all their details were closely followed in the façades of temples, as in other prestigious buildings. However the idealized proportions between the different elements set out by the only significant Roman writer on architecture to survive, <a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a>, and subsequent <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a> writers, do not reflect actual Roman practice, which could be very variable, though always aiming at balance and harmony. Following a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic" title="Hellenistic">Hellenistic</a> trend, the <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_order" title="Corinthian order">Corinthian order</a> and its variant the <a href="/wiki/Composite_order" title="Composite order">Composite order</a> were most common in surviving Roman temples, but for small temples like <a href="/wiki/Roman_temple_of_Alc%C3%A1ntara" title="Roman temple of Alcántara">that at Alcántara</a>, a simple <a href="/wiki/Tuscan_order" title="Tuscan order">Tuscan order</a> could be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>There was considerable local variation in style, as Roman architects often tried to incorporate elements the population expected in its <a href="/wiki/Sacred_architecture" title="Sacred architecture">sacred architecture</a>. This was especially the case in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>, where different traditions of large stone temples were already millennia old. The <a href="/wiki/Romano-Celtic_temple" title="Romano-Celtic temple">Romano-Celtic temple</a> was a simple style for small temples found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Empire" title="Western Empire">Western Empire</a>, and by far the most common type in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a>. It often lacked any of the distinctive classical features, and may have had considerable continuity with pre-Roman temples of the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_polytheism" title="Celtic polytheism">Celtic religion</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theatres">Theatres</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Theatres">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Roman_theatre_(structure)" title="Roman theatre (structure)">Roman theatres</a> were built in all areas of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">empire</a> from Spain, to the Middle East. Because of the Romans' ability to influence local architecture, we see numerous theatres around the world with uniquely Roman attributes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson_Jones2000_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson_Jones2000-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>These buildings were semi-circular and possessed certain inherent architectural structures, with minor differences depending on the region in which they were constructed. The <i><a href="/wiki/Scaenae_frons" title="Scaenae frons">scaenae frons</a></i> was a high back wall of the stage floor, supported by columns. The <i><a href="/wiki/Proscenium" title="Proscenium">proscaenium</a></i> was a wall that supported the front edge of the stage with ornately decorated niches off to the sides. The Hellenistic influence is seen through the use of the <i><a href="/wiki/Proscenium" title="Proscenium">proscaenium</a></i>. The Roman theatre also had a <i><a href="/wiki/Podium" title="Podium">podium</a></i>, which sometimes supported the columns of the <i>scaenae frons</i>. The <i>scaenae</i> was originally not part of the building itself, constructed only to provide sufficient background for the actors. Eventually, it became a part of the edifice itself, made out of concrete. The theatre itself was divided into the stage (<a href="/wiki/Orchestra" title="Orchestra">orchestra</a>) and the seating section (<a href="/wiki/Auditorium" title="Auditorium">auditorium</a>). <i><a href="/wiki/Vomitorium" title="Vomitorium">Vomitoria</a></i> or entrances and exits were made available to the audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERos1996_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERos1996-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Villa">Villa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Villa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Villa_of_the_Mysteries_in_Pompeii.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Villa_of_the_Mysteries_in_Pompeii.jpg/300px-Villa_of_the_Mysteries_in_Pompeii.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Villa_of_the_Mysteries_in_Pompeii.jpg/450px-Villa_of_the_Mysteries_in_Pompeii.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Villa_of_the_Mysteries_in_Pompeii.jpg/600px-Villa_of_the_Mysteries_in_Pompeii.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Villa_of_the_Mysteries_in_Pompeii.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Villa_of_the_Mysteries" title="Villa of the Mysteries">Villa of the Mysteries</a> just outside <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>, seen from above</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roman_villa" title="Roman villa">Roman villa</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Villa_rustica" title="Villa rustica">Villa rustica</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_villas_in_England" title="List of Roman villas in England">List of Roman villas in England</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_villas_in_Belgium" title="List of Roman villas in Belgium">List of Roman villas in Belgium</a></div>
<p>A Roman villa was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Country_house" title="Country house">country house</a> built for the upper class, while a <a href="/wiki/Domus" title="Domus">domus</a> was a wealthy family's house in a town. The Empire contained many kinds of villas, not all of them lavishly appointed with <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a> floors and <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">frescoes</a>. In the provinces, any country house with some decorative features in the Roman style may be called a "villa" by modern scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard-Perkins2000333_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard-Perkins2000333-48">[48]</a></sup> Some were pleasure palaces such as those— like <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Villa" title="Hadrian's Villa">Hadrian's Villa</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tivoli,_Italy" title="Tivoli, Italy">Tivoli</a>— that were situated in the cool hills within easy reach of <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> or— like the <a href="/wiki/Villa_of_the_Papyri" title="Villa of the Papyri">Villa of the Papyri</a> at <a href="/wiki/Herculaneum" title="Herculaneum">Herculaneum</a>— on picturesque sites overlooking the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bay_of_Naples" title="Bay of Naples">Bay of Naples</a>. Some villas were more like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Country_house" title="Country house">country houses</a> of England or <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, the visible seat of power of a local magnate, such as the famous palace rediscovered at <a href="/wiki/Fishbourne_Roman_Palace" title="Fishbourne Roman Palace">Fishbourne</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sussex" title="Sussex">Sussex</a>.</p>
<p>Suburban villas on the edge of cities were also known, such as the Middle and Late Republican villas that encroached on the <a href="/wiki/Campus_Martius" title="Campus Martius">Campus Martius</a>, at that time on the edge of Rome, and which can be also seen outside the city walls of <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Villa_of_the_Mysteries" title="Villa of the Mysteries">Villa of the Mysteries</a>, famous for its frescos. These early suburban villas, such as the one at Rome's Auditorium site<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> or at Grottarossa in Rome, demonstrate the antiquity and heritage of the <i>villa suburbana</i> in Central Italy. It is possible that these early, suburban villas were also in fact the seats of power (maybe even palaces) of regional strongmen or heads of important families (<i>gentes</i>).</p>
<p>A third type of villa provided the organizational center of the large farming estates called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latifundia" title="Latifundia">latifundia</a>; such villas might be lacking in luxuries. By the 4th century, <i>villa</i> could simply mean an agricultural estate or holding: <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> translated the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Mark" title="Gospel of Mark">Gospel of Mark</a> (xiv, 32) <i>chorion</i>, describing the olive grove of <a href="/wiki/Gethsemane" title="Gethsemane">Gethsemane</a>, with <i>villa</i>, without an inference that there were any dwellings there at all (<i>Catholic Encyclopedia</i> "Gethsemane").</p>
<p>With the colossal <a href="/wiki/Diocletian%27s_Palace" title="Diocletian's Palace">Diocletian's Palace</a>, built in the countryside but later turned into a fortified city, a form of residential <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">castle</a> emerges, that anticipates the Middle Ages.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Watermills">Watermills</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Watermills">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_ancient_watermills" title="List of ancient watermills">List of ancient watermills</a></div>
<p>The initial invention of the <a href="/wiki/Watermill" title="Watermill">watermill</a> appears to have occurred in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" title="Hellenistic civilization">hellenized eastern Mediterranean</a> in the wake of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conquests_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Conquests of Alexander the Great">conquests of Alexander the Great</a> and the rise of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_science_in_Classical_Antiquity#Hellenistic_period" title="History of science in Classical Antiquity">Hellenistic science and technology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWikander2000a396f._50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWikander2000a396f.-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonnersWaelkensDeckers200211_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonnersWaelkensDeckers200211-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson20027f._52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson20027f.-52">[52]</a></sup> In the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> era, the use of water-power was diversified and different types of watermills were introduced. These include all three variants of the vertical <a href="/wiki/Water_wheel" title="Water wheel">water wheel</a> as well as the horizontal water wheel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWikander2000a373.E2.80.93378_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWikander2000a373.E2.80.93378-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonnersWaelkensDeckers200212.E2.80.9315_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonnersWaelkensDeckers200212.E2.80.9315-54">[54]</a></sup> Apart from its main use in grinding flour, water-power was also applied to pounding grain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWikander1985158_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWikander1985158-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWikander2000b403_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWikander2000b403-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson200216_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson200216-57">[57]</a></sup> crushing ore,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWikander2000b407_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWikander2000b407-58">[58]</a></sup> sawing stones<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERittiGreweKessener2007_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERittiGreweKessener2007-59">[59]</a></sup> and possibly fulling and bellows for iron furnaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWikander2000b406f._60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWikander2000b406f.-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Decorative_structures">Decorative structures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Decorative structures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monoliths">Monoliths</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Monoliths">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_ancient_Greek_and_Roman_monoliths" title="List of ancient Greek and Roman monoliths">List of ancient Greek and Roman monoliths</a></div>
<p>In architecture, a <a href="/wiki/Monolithic_architecture" title="Monolithic architecture">monolith</a> is a structure which has been excavated as a unit from a surrounding matrix or outcropping of rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Monoliths are found in all types of Roman buildings. They were either: quarried without being moved; or quarried and moved; or quarried, moved and lifted clear off the ground into their position (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Architrave" title="Architrave">architraves</a>); or quarried, moved and erected in an upright position (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Column" title="Column">columns</a>).</p>
<p>Transporting was done by land or water (or a combination of both), in the later case often by special-built ships such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obelisk_carrier" title="Obelisk carrier">obelisk carriers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWirsching2000_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWirsching2000-62">[62]</a></sup> For lifting operations, <a href="/wiki/Crane_(machine)#History" title="Crane (machine)">ancient cranes</a> were employed since ca. 515 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulton19747.2C_16_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulton19747.2C_16-63">[63]</a></sup> such as in the <a href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_Column#Construction" title="Trajan's Column">construction of Trajan's Column</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELancaster1999419.E2.80.93439_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELancaster1999419.E2.80.93439-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Obelisks">Obelisks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Obelisks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_obelisks_in_Rome" title="List of obelisks in Rome">List of obelisks in Rome</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RomaObeliscoSallustiano.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="834" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/RomaObeliscoSallustiano.JPG/110px-RomaObeliscoSallustiano.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/RomaObeliscoSallustiano.JPG/165px-RomaObeliscoSallustiano.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/RomaObeliscoSallustiano.JPG/220px-RomaObeliscoSallustiano.JPG 2x" width="110"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RomaObeliscoSallustiano.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Obelisco Sallustiano</i> in front of the church of <a href="/wiki/Trinit%C3%A0_dei_Monti" title="Trinità dei Monti">Trinità dei Monti</a> in Rome</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Obelisk" title="Obelisk">obelisk</a> is a tall, four-sided, narrow tapering monument which ends in a pyramid-like shape at the top. These were originally called "tekhenu" by the builders, the ancient Egyptians. The Greeks who saw them used the Greek 'obeliskos' to describe them, and this word passed into Latin and then English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBakerBaker200169_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBakerBaker200169-65">[65]</a></sup> The Romans commissioned obelisks in an ancient Egyptian style. Examples include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Arles, France – the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arles_Obelisk" title="Arles Obelisk">Arles Obelisk</a>, in Place de la République, a 4th-century obelisk of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> origin</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Benevento" title="Benevento">Benevento</a>, Italy – three Roman obelisks<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></li>
<li>Munich – obelisk of Titus Sextius Africanus, <a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Staatliches_Museum_%C3%84gyptischer_Kunst" title="de:Staatliches Museum Ägyptischer Kunst">Staatliches Museum Ägyptischer Kunst</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kunstareal" title="Kunstareal">Kunstareal</a>, 1st century AD, 5.80 m</li>
<li>Rome – there are five ancient Roman obelisks in Rome.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_gardens">Roman gardens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Roman gardens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P1080474_Mosaico_Conimbriga_(Condeixa-a-Nova).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/P1080474_Mosaico_Conimbriga_%28Condeixa-a-Nova%29.jpg/220px-P1080474_Mosaico_Conimbriga_%28Condeixa-a-Nova%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/P1080474_Mosaico_Conimbriga_%28Condeixa-a-Nova%29.jpg/330px-P1080474_Mosaico_Conimbriga_%28Condeixa-a-Nova%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/P1080474_Mosaico_Conimbriga_%28Condeixa-a-Nova%29.jpg/440px-P1080474_Mosaico_Conimbriga_%28Condeixa-a-Nova%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:P1080474_Mosaico_Conimbriga_(Condeixa-a-Nova).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gardens in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conimbriga" title="Conimbriga">Conimbriga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Roman_gardens" title="Roman gardens">Roman gardens</a> were influenced by Egyptian, <a href="/wiki/Persian_gardens" title="Persian gardens">Persian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Greek_gardens" title="Greek gardens">Greek</a> gardening techniques. In Ancient <a href="/wiki/Latium" title="Latium">Latium</a>, a garden was part of every farm. According to <a href="/wiki/Cato_the_Elder" title="Cato the Elder">Cato the Elder</a>, every garden should be close to the house and should have flower beds and ornamental trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESemple1929435_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESemple1929435-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a> wrote that during his time <a href="/wiki/Flower_garden" title="Flower garden">flower gardens</a> became a national indulgence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESemple1929436_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESemple1929436-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gardens were not reserved for the extremely wealthy. Excavations in <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a> show that gardens attaching to residences were scaled down to meet the space constraints of the home of the average Roman. Modified versions of Roman garden designs were adopted in Roman settlements in <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)" title="Africa (Roman province)">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Gaul" title="Roman Gaul">Gaul</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Britannia</a>. As town houses were replaced by tall <i><a href="/wiki/Insula_(building)" title="Insula (building)">insula</a></i> (apartment buildings), these urban gardens were replaced by <a href="/wiki/Flower_box" title="Flower box">window boxes</a> or <a href="/wiki/Roof_garden" title="Roof garden">roof gardens</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Triumphal_arch">Triumphal arch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Triumphal arch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_triumphal_arches" title="List of Roman triumphal arches">List of Roman triumphal arches</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arch_of_titus_2.jpg"><img alt="Photograph" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Arch_of_titus_2.jpg/220px-Arch_of_titus_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Arch_of_titus_2.jpg/330px-Arch_of_titus_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Arch_of_titus_2.jpg/440px-Arch_of_titus_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The <a href="/wiki/Arch_of_Titus" title="Arch of Titus">Arch of Titus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, an early Roman imperial triumphal arch with a single archway</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fra-titusbuen.jpg"><img alt="Close-up photograph of arch ornamentation" data-file-height="1712" data-file-width="2288" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Fra-titusbuen.jpg/220px-Fra-titusbuen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Fra-titusbuen.jpg/330px-Fra-titusbuen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Fra-titusbuen.jpg/440px-Fra-titusbuen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Titus" title="Titus">Titus</a>' triumphal procession depicted on the Arch of Titus, showing the loot captured from <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> in 81 AD</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Triumphal_arch" title="Triumphal arch">triumphal arch</a> is a monumental structure in the shape of an <a href="/wiki/Arch" title="Arch">archway</a> with one or more arched passageways, often designed to span a road. The origins of the Roman triumphal arch are unclear. There were precursors to the triumphal arch within the Roman world; in Italy, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etruscans" title="Etruscans">Etruscans</a> used elaborately decorated single bay arches as gates or portals to their cities. Surviving examples of Etruscan arches can still be seen at <a href="/wiki/Perugia" title="Perugia">Perugia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volterra" title="Volterra">Volterra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaho200418.E2.80.9325_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaho200418.E2.80.9325-70">[70]</a></sup> The two key elements of the triumphal arch – a round-topped arch and a square entablature – had long been in use as separate architectural elements in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a>.</p>
<p>The innovation of the Romans was to these elements in a single free-standing structure. The columns became purely decorative elements on the outer face of arch, while the entablature, liberated from its role as a building support, became the frame for the civic and religious messages that the arch builders wished to convey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESullivan2006133.E2.80.93134_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESullivan2006133.E2.80.93134-71">[71]</a></sup> Little is known about how the Romans viewed triumphal arches. <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, writing in the first century AD, was the only ancient author to discuss them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEF.C3.BCrstGrundmann199843_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEF.C3.BCrstGrundmann199843-72">[72]</a></sup> He wrote that they were intended to "elevate above the ordinary world" an image of an honoured person usually depicted in the form of a statue with a <a href="/wiki/Quadriga" title="Quadriga">quadriga</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEF.C3.BCrstGrundmann199843_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEF.C3.BCrstGrundmann199843-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first recorded Roman triumphal arches were set up in the time of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-73">[73]</a></sup> Generals who were granted a <a href="/wiki/Roman_triumph" title="Roman triumph">triumph</a> were termed <i>triumphators</i> and would erect <i><a href="/wiki/Arch" title="Arch">fornices</a></i> or honorific arches bearing statues to commemorate their victories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grove_Art_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grove_Art-74">[74]</a></sup> Roman triumphal practices changed significantly at the start of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">imperial period</a> when the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Emperor" title="Roman Emperor">Roman Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> decreed that only emperors would be granted <a href="/wiki/Roman_triumph" title="Roman triumph">triumphs</a>. The triumphal arch changed from being a personal monument to being an essentially propagandistic one, serving to announce and promote the presence of the ruler and the laws of the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaho200418.E2.80.9325_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaho200418.E2.80.9325-70">[70]</a></sup> Arches were not necessarily built as entrances, but – unlike many modern triumphal arches – they were often erected across roads and were intended to be passed through, not round.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHonourFleming2005_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHonourFleming2005-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most Roman triumphal arches were built during the imperial period. By the fourth century AD there were 36 such arches in Rome, of which three have survived – the <a href="/wiki/Arch_of_Titus" title="Arch of Titus">Arch of Titus</a> (AD 81), the <a href="/wiki/Arch_of_Septimius_Severus" title="Arch of Septimius Severus">Arch of Septimius Severus</a> (203-205) and the <a href="/wiki/Arch_of_Constantine" title="Arch of Constantine">Arch of Constantine</a> (312). Numerous arches were built elsewhere in the Roman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-73">[73]</a></sup> The single arch was the most common, but many triple arches were also built, of which the <a href="/wiki/Triumphal_Arch_of_Orange" title="Triumphal Arch of Orange">Triumphal Arch of Orange</a> (<i>circa</i> AD 21) is the earliest surviving example. From the 2nd century AD, many examples of the <i>arcus quadrifrons</i> – a square triumphal arch erected over a crossroads, with arched openings on all four sides – were built, especially in North Africa. Arch-building in Rome and Italy diminished after the time of Trajan (AD 98-117) but remained widespread in the provinces during the 2nd and 3rd centuries AD; they were often erected to commemorate imperial visits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grove_Art_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grove_Art-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ornamentation of an arch was intended to serve as a constant visual reminder of the triumph and <i>triumphator</i>. The façade was ornamented with marble columns, and the piers and attics with decorative cornices. Sculpted panels depicted victories and achievements, the deeds of the <i>triumphator</i>, the captured weapons of the enemy or the triumphal procession itself. The <a href="/wiki/Spandrel" title="Spandrel">spandrels</a> usually depicted flying <a href="/wiki/Victoria_(mythology)" title="Victoria (mythology)">Victories</a>, while the attic was often inscribed with a dedicatory inscription naming and praising the <i>triumphator</i>. The piers and internal passageways were also decorated with reliefs and free-standing sculptures. The vault was ornamented with <a href="/wiki/Coffer" title="Coffer">coffers</a>. Some triumphal arches were surmounted by a statue or a <i>currus triumphalis</i>, a group of statues depicting the emperor or general in a <a href="/wiki/Quadriga" title="Quadriga">quadriga</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaho200418.E2.80.9325_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaho200418.E2.80.9325-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grove_Art_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-Grove_Art-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Inscriptions on Roman triumphal arches were works of art in themselves, with very finely cut, sometimes gilded letters. The form of each letter and the spacing between them was carefully designed for maximum clarity and simplicity, without any decorative flourishes, emphasizing the Roman taste for restraint and order. This conception of what later became the art of <a href="/wiki/Typography" title="Typography">typography</a> remains of fundamental importance down to the present day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHonourFleming2005_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHonourFleming2005-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Victory_columns">Victory columns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Victory columns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_victory_columns" title="List of Roman victory columns">List of Roman victory columns</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">Infrastructure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Infrastructure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PompeiiStreet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="795" data-file-width="594" height="335" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/PompeiiStreet.jpg/250px-PompeiiStreet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/PompeiiStreet.jpg/375px-PompeiiStreet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/PompeiiStreet.jpg/500px-PompeiiStreet.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PompeiiStreet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Roman street in <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roads">Roads</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Roads">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_road" title="Roman road">Roman roads</a> were vital to the maintenance and development of the Roman state, and were built from about 500 BC through the expansion and consolidation of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForbes1993146_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForbes1993146-76">[76]</a></sup> They provided efficient means for the overland movement of <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_ancient_Rome" title="Military history of ancient Rome">armies</a>, officials and civilians, and the inland carriage of official communications and <a href="/wiki/Roman_commerce" title="Roman commerce">trade</a> goods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaszynski20009_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaszynski20009-77">[77]</a></sup> At the peak of Rome's development, no fewer than 29 great military highways radiated from the capital, and the Late Empire's 113 provinces were interconnected by 372 great road links.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBunson2009195_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBunson2009195-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO.27Flaherty20022_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO.27Flaherty20022-79">[79]</a></sup> Roman road builders aimed at a regulation width (see <a href="#Laws_and_standards">Laws and standards</a> above), but actual widths have been measured at between 3.6 ft (1.1 m) and more than 23 ft (7.0 m). Today, the concrete has worn from the spaces around the stones, giving the impression of a very bumpy road, but the original practice was to produce a surface that was no doubt much closer to being flat.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aqueduct">Aqueduct</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Aqueduct">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roman_aqueduct" title="Roman aqueduct">Roman aqueduct</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_aqueducts_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="List of aqueducts in the Roman Empire">List of aqueducts in the Roman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AcueductoSegovia_edit1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1324" data-file-width="1746" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/AcueductoSegovia_edit1.jpg/220px-AcueductoSegovia_edit1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/AcueductoSegovia_edit1.jpg/330px-AcueductoSegovia_edit1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/AcueductoSegovia_edit1.jpg/440px-AcueductoSegovia_edit1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AcueductoSegovia_edit1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Aqueduct_of_Segovia" title="Aqueduct of Segovia">Aqueduct of Segovia</a>, Spain</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> constructed numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aqueduct_(watercourse)" title="Aqueduct (watercourse)">aqueducts</a> in order to bring water from distant sources into their cities and towns, supplying <a href="/wiki/Thermae" title="Thermae">public baths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latrine" title="Latrine">latrines</a>, fountains and private households. Waste water was removed by complex <a href="/wiki/Sanitation_in_ancient_Rome" title="Sanitation in ancient Rome">sewage systems</a> and released into nearby bodies of water, keeping the towns clean and free from effluent. Aqueducts also provided water for mining operations, milling, farms and gardens.</p>
<p>Aqueducts moved water through gravity alone, being constructed along a slight downward gradient within conduits of stone, brick or concrete. Most were buried beneath the ground, and followed its contours; obstructing peaks were circumvented or, less often, tunnelled through. Where valleys or lowlands intervened, the conduit was carried on bridgework, or its contents fed into high-pressure lead, ceramic or stone pipes and siphoned across. Most aqueduct systems included sedimentation tanks, <a href="/wiki/Sluice" title="Sluice">sluices</a> and distribution tanks to regulate the supply at need.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>'s first aqueduct supplied a water-fountain sited at the city's cattle market. By the third century AD, the city had eleven aqueducts, sustaining a population of over a million in a water-extravagant economy; most of the water supplied the city's many public baths. Cities and municipalities throughout the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> emulated this model, and funded aqueducts as objects of public interest and civic pride, "an expensive yet necessary luxury to which all could, and did, aspire."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGagarinFantham2010145_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGagarinFantham2010145-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most Roman aqueducts proved reliable, and durable; some were maintained into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_modern" title="Early modern">early modern</a> era, and a few are still partly in use. Methods of aqueduct surveying and construction are noted by <a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a> in his work <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Architectura" title="De Architectura">De Architectura</a></i> (1st century BC). The general <a href="/wiki/Frontinus" title="Frontinus">Frontinus</a> gives more detail in his <a href="/wiki/De_aquaeductu" title="De aquaeductu">official report</a> on the problems, uses and abuses of Imperial Rome's public water supply. Notable examples of aqueduct architecture include the supporting piers of the <a href="/wiki/Aqueduct_of_Segovia" title="Aqueduct of Segovia">Aqueduct of Segovia</a>, and the aqueduct-fed cisterns of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bridges">Bridges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Bridges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bridge_Alcantara.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Bridge_Alcantara.JPG/300px-Bridge_Alcantara.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Bridge_Alcantara.JPG/450px-Bridge_Alcantara.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Bridge_Alcantara.JPG/600px-Bridge_Alcantara.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bridge_Alcantara.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Alc%C3%A1ntara_Bridge" title="Alcántara Bridge">Alcántara Bridge</a>, Spain, a masterpiece of ancient bridge building</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roman_bridge" title="Roman bridge">Roman bridge</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_bridges" title="List of Roman bridges">List of Roman bridges</a></div>
<p>Roman bridges, built by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Romans</a>, were the first large and lasting bridges built.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO.27Connor19931_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO.27Connor19931-81">[81]</a></sup> Roman bridges were built with stone and had the <a href="/wiki/Arch" title="Arch">arch</a> as the basic structure (see <a href="/wiki/Arch_bridge" title="Arch bridge">arch bridge</a>). Most utilized <a href="/wiki/Roman_concrete" title="Roman concrete">concrete</a> as well, which the Romans were the first to use for bridges.</p>
<p>Roman arch bridges were usually <a href="/wiki/Semicircle" title="Semicircle">semicircular</a>, although a few were <a href="/wiki/Circular_segment" title="Circular segment">segmental</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Alcon%C3%A9tar_Bridge" title="Alconétar Bridge">Alconétar Bridge</a>). A segmental arch is an arch that is less than a semicircle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> The advantages of the <a href="/wiki/Circular_segment" title="Circular segment">segmental</a> arch bridge were that it allowed great amounts of flood water to pass under it, which would prevent the bridge from being swept away during floods and the bridge itself could be more lightweight. Generally, Roman bridges featured wedge-shaped primary arch stones (<a href="/wiki/Voussoir" title="Voussoir">voussoirs</a>) of the same in size and shape. The Romans built both single spans and lengthy multiple arch <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_aqueducts" title="Roman aqueducts">aqueducts</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Pont_du_Gard" title="Pont du Gard">Pont du Gard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aqueduct_of_Segovia" title="Aqueduct of Segovia">Segovia Aqueduct</a>. Their bridges featured from an early time onwards flood openings in the piers, e.g. in the <a href="/wiki/Pons_Fabricius" title="Pons Fabricius">Pons Fabricius</a> in Rome (62 BC), one of the world's oldest major bridges still standing. Roman engineers were the first and until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a> the only ones to construct bridges with <a href="/wiki/Roman_concrete" title="Roman concrete">concrete</a>, which they called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opus_caementicium" title="Opus caementicium">Opus caementicium</a>. The outside was usually covered with brick or ashlar, as in the Alcántara bridge.</p>
<p>The Romans also introduced segmental arch bridges into bridge construction. The 330 m long <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Limyra_Bridge" title="Limyra Bridge">Limyra Bridge</a> in southwestern <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> features 26 segmental arches with an average span-to-rise ratio of 5.3:1,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO.27Connor1993126_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO.27Connor1993126-83">[83]</a></sup> giving the bridge an unusually flat profile unsurpassed for more than a millennium. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_bridge" title="Trajan's bridge">Trajan's bridge</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> featured open-spandrel segmental arches made of wood (standing on 40 m high concrete piers). This was to be the longest arch bridge for a thousand years both in terms of overall and individual span length, while the longest extant Roman bridge is the 790 m long <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puente_Romano_(M%C3%A9rida)" title="Puente Romano (Mérida)">Puente Romano</a> at <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9rida,_Spain" title="Mérida, Spain">Mérida</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canals">Canals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Canals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_canals" title="List of Roman canals">List of Roman canals</a></div>
<p>Roman canals were typically multi-purpose structures, intended for <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drainage" title="Drainage">drainage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Land_reclamation" title="Land reclamation">land reclamation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flood_control" title="Flood control">flood control</a> and <a href="/wiki/Navigation" title="Navigation">navigation</a> where feasible. Some navigational canals were recorded by <a href="/wiki/List_of_Graeco-Roman_geographers" title="List of Graeco-Roman geographers">ancient geographers</a> and are still traceable by modern archaeology. Channels which served the needs of urban <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">water supply</a> are covered at the <a href="/wiki/List_of_aqueducts_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="List of aqueducts in the Roman Empire">List of aqueducts in the Roman Empire</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cisterns">Cisterns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Cisterns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_cisterns" title="List of Roman cisterns">List of Roman cisterns</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yerebatan2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1713" data-file-width="2541" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Yerebatan2.JPG/220px-Yerebatan2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Yerebatan2.JPG/330px-Yerebatan2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Yerebatan2.JPG/440px-Yerebatan2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yerebatan2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Basilica_Cistern" title="Basilica Cistern">Basilica Cistern</a> in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> provided water for the <a href="/wiki/Great_Palace_of_Constantinople" title="Great Palace of Constantinople">Imperial Palace</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Freshwater reservoirs were commonly set up at the termini of <a href="/wiki/Roman_aqueduct" title="Roman aqueduct">aqueducts</a> and their branch lines, supplying urban households, <a href="/wiki/Villa_rustica" title="Villa rustica">agricultural estates</a>, imperial palaces, <a href="/wiki/Thermae" title="Thermae">thermae</a> or naval bases of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_navy" title="Roman navy">Roman navy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED.C3.B6ring2002310.E2.80.93319_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED.C3.B6ring2002310.E2.80.93319-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dams">Dams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Dams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_dams_and_reservoirs" title="List of Roman dams and reservoirs">List of Roman dams and reservoirs</a></div>
<p>Roman dam construction began in earnest in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="History of the Roman Empire">early imperial period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge199287_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge199287-85">[85]</a></sup> For the most part, it concentrated on the semi-arid fringe of the empire, namely the provinces of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_North_Africa" title="Roman North Africa">North Africa</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchnitter197828.2C_fig._7_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchnitter197828.2C_fig._7-86">[86]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge199280_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge199280-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge2000332_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge2000332-88">[88]</a></sup> The relative abundance of Spanish dams below is due partly to more intensive field work there; for <a href="/wiki/Roman_Italy" title="Roman Italy">Italy</a> only the <a href="/wiki/Subiaco_Dams" title="Subiaco Dams">Subiaco Dams</a>, created by emperor <a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a> (54–68 AD) for recreational purposes, are attested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith197060f._89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith197060f.-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge199287_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge199287-85">[85]</a></sup> These dams are noteworthy, though, for their extraordinary height, which remained unsurpassed anywhere in the world until the <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge199287_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge199287-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most frequent dam types were earth- or rock-filled <a href="/wiki/Embankment_dam" title="Embankment dam">embankment dams</a> and masonry <a href="/wiki/Gravity_dam" title="Gravity dam">gravity dams</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge2000331f._90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge2000331f.-90">[90]</a></sup> These served a wide array of purposes, such as <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flood_control" title="Flood control">flood control</a>, river diversion, soil-retention, or a combination of these functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge199286f._91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge199286f.-91">[91]</a></sup> The impermeability of Roman dams was increased by the introduction of waterproof hydraulic <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(masonry)" title="Mortar (masonry)">mortar</a> and especially <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opus_caementicium" title="Opus caementicium">opus caementicium</a></i> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concrete_Revolution" title="Concrete Revolution">Concrete Revolution</a>. These materials also allowed for bigger structures to be built,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith197149_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith197149-92">[92]</a></sup> like the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Homs_Dam" title="Lake Homs Dam">Lake Homs Dam</a>, possibly the largest water barrier today,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith197142_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith197142-93">[93]</a></sup> and the sturdy <a href="/wiki/Harbaqa_Dam" title="Harbaqa Dam">Harbaqa Dam</a>, both of which consist of a concrete core.</p>
<p>Roman builders were the first to realize the stabilizing effect of <a href="/wiki/Arch" title="Arch">arches</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buttress" title="Buttress">buttresses</a>, which they integrated into their dam designs. Previously unknown dam types introduced by the Romans include <a href="/wiki/Arch-gravity_dam" title="Arch-gravity dam">arch-gravity dams</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge2000332_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge2000332-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJamesChanson2002_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJamesChanson2002-94">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arch_dam" title="Arch dam">arch dams</a>,;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith197133.E2.80.9335_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith197133.E2.80.9335-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchnitter197831f._96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchnitter197831f.-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchnitter1987a12_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchnitter1987a12-97">[97]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchnitter1987c80_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchnitter1987c80-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge2000332.2C_fn._2_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge2000332.2C_fn._2-99">[99]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Buttress_dam" title="Buttress dam">buttress dams</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchnitter1987b59.E2.80.9362_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchnitter1987b59.E2.80.9362-100">[100]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Arch_dam#Variations" title="Arch dam">multiple-arch buttress dams</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchnitter197829_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchnitter197829-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchnitter1987b60.2C_table_1.2C_62_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchnitter1987b60.2C_table_1.2C_62-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJamesChanson2002_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJamesChanson2002-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArenillasCastillo2003_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArenillasCastillo2003-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defensive_walls">Defensive walls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Defensive walls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Servian_Wall-Termini_Station.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2032" data-file-width="1524" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Servian_Wall-Termini_Station.jpg/220px-Servian_Wall-Termini_Station.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Servian_Wall-Termini_Station.jpg/330px-Servian_Wall-Termini_Station.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Servian_Wall-Termini_Station.jpg/440px-Servian_Wall-Termini_Station.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Servian_Wall-Termini_Station.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Section of the <a href="/wiki/Servian_Wall" title="Servian Wall">Servian Wall</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trier_Porta_Nigra_BW_2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2516" data-file-width="3404" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Trier_Porta_Nigra_BW_2.JPG/220px-Trier_Porta_Nigra_BW_2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Trier_Porta_Nigra_BW_2.JPG/330px-Trier_Porta_Nigra_BW_2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Trier_Porta_Nigra_BW_2.JPG/440px-Trier_Porta_Nigra_BW_2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trier_Porta_Nigra_BW_2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Porta_Nigra" title="Porta Nigra">Porta Nigra</a> of <a href="/wiki/Trier" title="Trier">Trier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, capital of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Roman province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Belgica" title="Gallia Belgica">Gallia Belgica</a>, constructed between 186 and 200 AD</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_defensive_walls" title="Ancient Roman defensive walls">Ancient Roman defensive walls</a></div>
<p>The Romans generally fortified cities, rather than fortresses, but there are some fortified camps, such as the <a href="/wiki/Saxon_Shore#The_forts" title="Saxon Shore">Saxon Shore forts</a> like <a href="/wiki/Portus_Adurni" title="Portus Adurni">Porchester Castle</a> in England. City walls were already significant in <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_architecture" title="Etruscan architecture">Etruscan architecture</a>, and in the struggle for control of Italy under the early Republic many more were built, using different techniques. These included tightly-fitting massive irregular polygonal blocks, shaped to fit exactly in a way reminiscent of later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inca" title="Inca">Inca</a> work. The Romans called a simple rampart wall an <a href="/wiki/Agger_(ancient_Rome)" title="Agger (ancient Rome)">agger</a>; at this date great height was not necessary. The <a href="/wiki/Servian_Wall" title="Servian Wall">Servian Wall</a> around Rome was an ambitious project of the early 4th century BC. The wall was up to 10 metres (32.8 ft) in height in places, 3.6 metres (12 ft) wide at its base, 11 km (7 mi) long,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> and is believed to have had 16 main gates, though many of these are mentioned only from writings, with no other known remains. Some of it had a <i>fossa</i> or ditch in front, and an agger behind, and it was enough to deter <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a>. Later the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurelian_Wall" title="Aurelian Wall">Aurelian Wall</a> replaced it, enclosing an expanded city, and using more sophisticated designs, with small forts at intervals.</p>
<p>The Romans walled major cities and towns in areas they saw as vulnerable, and parts of many walls remain incorporated in later defences, as at <a href="/wiki/Roman_walls_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Roman walls of Córdoba">Córdoba</a> (2nd century BC), <a href="/wiki/Chester_city_walls" title="Chester city walls">Chester</a> (earth and wood in the 70s AD, stone from c. 100), and <a href="/wiki/York_city_walls" title="York city walls">York</a> (from 70s AD). Strategic walls across open country were far rarer, and <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian's Wall">Hadrian's Wall</a> (from 122) and the <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Wall" title="Antonine Wall">Antonine Wall</a> (from 142, abandoned only 8 years after completion) are the most significant examples, both on the <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Pictish</a> frontier.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architectural_features">Architectural features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Architectural features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mosaics">Mosaics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Mosaics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On his return from campaigns in Greece, the general <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucius_Cornelius_Sulla" title="Lucius Cornelius Sulla">Sulla</a> brought back what is probably the most well-known element of the early <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">imperial period</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a>, a decoration made of colourful chips of stone inserted into cement. This tiling method took the empire by storm in the late first century and the second century and in the Roman home joined the well known <a href="/wiki/Mural" title="Mural">mural</a> in decorating floors, walls, and <a href="/wiki/Grotto" title="Grotto">grottoes</a> with <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Image" title="Image">pictorial</a> designs.</p>
<p>There were two main techniques in Greco-Roman mosaic: <i><a href="/wiki/Opus_vermiculatum" title="Opus vermiculatum">opus vermiculatum</a></i> used tiny <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tesserae" title="Tesserae">tesserae</a></i>, typically cubes of 4 millimeters or less, and was produced in workshops in relatively small panels which were transported to the site glued to some temporary support. The tiny <i>tesserae</i> allowed very fine detail, and an approach to the illusionism of painting. Often small panels called <i>emblemata</i> were inserted into walls or as the highlights of larger floor-mosaics in coarser work. The normal technique, however, was <i><a href="/wiki/Opus_tessellatum" title="Opus tessellatum">opus tessellatum</a></i>, using larger tesserae, which were laid on site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1983116.E2.80.93119_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1983116.E2.80.93119-105">[105]</a></sup> There was a distinct native Italian style using black on a white background, which was no doubt cheaper than fully coloured work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1983121.E2.80.93123_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1983121.E2.80.93123-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>A specific genre of Roman mosaic obtained the name <i>asaroton</i> (Greek "unswept floor"). It represented an optical illusion of the leftovers from a feast on the floor of reach houses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1972_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1972-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hypocaust">Hypocaust</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Hypocaust">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vieux_la_Romaine_Villa_hypocauste.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Vieux_la_Romaine_Villa_hypocauste.jpg/300px-Vieux_la_Romaine_Villa_hypocauste.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Vieux_la_Romaine_Villa_hypocauste.jpg/450px-Vieux_la_Romaine_Villa_hypocauste.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Vieux_la_Romaine_Villa_hypocauste.jpg/600px-Vieux_la_Romaine_Villa_hypocauste.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vieux_la_Romaine_Villa_hypocauste.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Part-excavated <a href="/wiki/Hypocaust" title="Hypocaust">hypocaust</a> under the floor in a <a href="/wiki/Roman_villa" title="Roman villa">Roman villa</a> in Vieux-la-Romaine, near <a href="/wiki/Caen" title="Caen">Caen</a>, France</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Hypocaust" title="Hypocaust">hypocaust</a> was an ancient Roman system of <a href="/wiki/Underfloor_heating" title="Underfloor heating">underfloor heating</a>, used to heat houses with hot air. The Roman architect Vitruvius, writing about the end of the 1st century B.C., attributes their invention to <a href="/wiki/Sergius_Orata" title="Sergius Orata">Sergius Orata</a>. Many remains of Roman hypocausts have survived throughout Europe, western Asia, and northern Africa. The hypocaust was an invention which improved the hygiene and living conditions of citizens, and was a forerunner of modern <a href="/wiki/Central_heating" title="Central heating">central heating</a>.</p>
<p>Hypocausts were used for heating hot baths (<a href="/wiki/Thermae" title="Thermae">thermae</a>), houses and other buildings, whether public or private. The floor was raised above the ground by pillars, called <a href="/wiki/Pilae_stacks" title="Pilae stacks">pilae stacks</a>, with a layer of tiles, then a layer of concrete, then another of tiles on top; and spaces were left inside the walls so that hot air and smoke from the <a href="/wiki/Furnace" title="Furnace">furnace</a> would pass through these enclosed areas and out of flues in the roof, thereby heating but not polluting the interior of the room.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_roofs">Roman roofs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Roman roofs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_ancient_Greek_and_Roman_roofs" title="List of ancient Greek and Roman roofs">List of ancient Greek and Roman roofs</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Magna_Graecia" title="Magna Graecia">Sicily</a> truss roofs presumably appeared as early as 550 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge196038.E2.80.9344_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge196038.E2.80.9344-108">[108]</a></sup>Their potential was fully realized in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> period, which saw trussed roofs over 30 wide spanning the rectangular spaces of monumental public buildings such as <a href="/wiki/Roman_temple" title="Roman temple">temples</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilicas</a>, and later <a href="/wiki/Church_(building)" title="Church (building)">churches</a>. Such spans were three times as wide as the widest prop-and-lintel roofs and only surpassed by the largest <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_domes" title="List of Roman domes">Roman domes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUlrich2007148f_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUlrich2007148f-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>The largest <a href="/wiki/Truss" title="Truss">truss</a> roof by span of ancient Rome covered the <a href="/wiki/Flavian_Palace" title="Flavian Palace">Aula Regia</a> (throne room) built for emperor <a href="/wiki/Domitian" title="Domitian">Domitian</a> (81–96 AD) on the <a href="/wiki/Palatine_Hill" title="Palatine Hill">Palatine Hill</a>, Rome. The timber truss roof had a width of 31.67 m, slightly surpassing the postulated limit of 30 m for Roman roof constructions. Tie-beam trusses allowed for much larger spans than the older prop-and-lintel system and even concrete vaulting. Nine out of the ten largest rectangular spaces in Roman architecture were bridged this way, the only exception being the groin vaulted <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Maxentius" title="Basilica of Maxentius">Basilica of Maxentius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUlrich2007148f_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUlrich2007148f-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spiral_stairs">Spiral stairs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Spiral stairs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_ancient_spiral_stairs" title="List of ancient spiral stairs">List of ancient spiral stairs</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stairway#Spiral_and_helical_stairs" title="Stairway">spiral stair</a> is a type of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stairway" title="Stairway">stairway</a> which, due to its complex <a href="/wiki/Helix" title="Helix">helical</a> structure, was introduced relatively late into architecture. Although the oldest example dates back to the 5th century BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckmann2002_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckmann2002-110">[110]</a></sup> it was only in the wake of the influential design of <a href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_Column" title="Trajan's Column">Trajan's Column</a> that this space-saving new type permanently caught hold in Roman architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckmann2002353.E2.80.93356_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckmann2002353.E2.80.93356-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Apart from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triumphal_column" title="Triumphal column">triumphal columns</a> in the imperial cities of <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, other types of buildings such as <a href="/wiki/Roman_temple" title="Roman temple">temples</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thermae" title="Thermae">thermae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilicas</a> and tombs were also fitted with spiral stairways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckmann2002353.E2.80.93356_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckmann2002353.E2.80.93356-111">[111]</a></sup> Their notable absence in the towers of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurelian_Wall" title="Aurelian Wall">Aurelian Wall</a> indicates that although used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_castle" title="Medieval castle">medieval castles</a>, they did not yet figure prominently in <a href="/wiki/Roman_military_engineering" title="Roman military engineering">Roman military engineering</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckmann2002353.E2.80.93356_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckmann2002353.E2.80.93356-111">[111]</a></sup> By <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_antiquity" title="Late antiquity">late antiquity</a>, separate stair towers were constructed adjacent to the main buildings, as in the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_San_Vitale" title="Basilica of San Vitale">Basilica of San Vitale</a>.</p>
<p>The construction of spiral stairs passed on both to <a href="/wiki/Church_architecture" title="Church architecture">Christian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic architecture</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Significant_buildings_and_areas">Significant buildings and areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Significant buildings and areas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_buildings">Public buildings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Public buildings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baths_of_Trajan" title="Baths of Trajan">Baths of Trajan</a> – these were a massive <a href="/wiki/Thermae" title="Thermae"><i>thermae</i>, a bathing and leisure complex</a>, built in ancient Rome starting from 104 AD and dedicated during the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalends" title="Kalends">Kalends</a></i> of July in 109.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baths_of_Diocletian" title="Baths of Diocletian">Baths of Diocletian</a> – in ancient Rome, these were the grandest of the public baths (thermae), built by successive emperors</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baths_of_Caracalla" title="Baths of Caracalla">Baths of Caracalla</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Colosseum" title="Colosseum">Colosseum</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_Column" title="Trajan's Column">Trajan's Column</a>, in Rome</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Circus_Maximus" title="Circus Maximus">Circus Maximus</a>, in Rome</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Curia_Hostilia" title="Curia Hostilia">Curia Hostilia</a> (Senate House), in Rome</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Domus_Aurea" title="Domus Aurea">Domus Aurea</a> (former building)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Pantheon</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tower_of_Hercules" title="Tower of Hercules">Tower of Hercules</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tropaeum_Traiani" title="Tropaeum Traiani">Tropaeum Traiani</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Villa" title="Hadrian's Villa">Hadrian's Villa</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verona_Arena" title="Verona Arena">Verona Arena</a>, in Verona</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Private_architecture">Private architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Private architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alyscamps" title="Alyscamps">Alyscamps</a> – a <a href="/wiki/Necropolis" title="Necropolis">necropolis</a> in <a href="/wiki/Arles" title="Arles">Arles</a>, France, one of the most famous necropolises of the ancient world</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Domus" title="Domus">Domus</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Catacombs_of_Rome" title="Catacombs of Rome">Catacombs of Rome</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Roman_villa" title="Roman villa">Roman villa</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herculaneum" title="Herculaneum">Herculaneum</a></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_engineering">Civil engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Roman_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Civil engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Roman_engineering" title="Roman engineering">Roman engineering</a> – Romans are famous for their advanced <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a> accomplishments, although some of their own inventions were improvements on older ideas, concepts and inventions.</li>

</ul>
